Here's the single command to run remaster.py remotely with wget and python3:

wget -O - https://raw.githubusercontent.com/YOUR_USERNAME/YOUR_REPO/main/remaster.py | python3
However, since I don't know your GitHub username and repository name, here are the alternatives:

Option 1: If you have the file hosted somewhere:

wget -O - https://your-domain.com/remaster.py | python3
Option 2: If you want to download and run in one step:

wget https://your-domain.com/remaster.py && python3 remaster.py
Option 3: Using curl instead of wget:

curl -s https://your-domain.com/remaster.py | python3
Option 4: If you want to download, make executable, and run:

wget https://your-domain.com/remaster.py && chmod +x remaster.py && python3 remaster.py
The wget -O - pipes the content directly to python3, while wget without -O - downloads the file first. Choose the option that works best for your hosting setup.

create remaster.py
- before any sudo password prompts, it will post a version number (starting with 0.00.1) that you will increment with each edit.
- it will download https://mirror.pilotfiber.com/ubuntu-iso/24.04.2/ubuntu-24.04.2-live-server-amd64.iso
- no matter how small the download is, the download progress will always show a live status of the download.

wget -O - https://your-domain.com/remaster.py | python3
curl -s https://your-domain.com/remaster.py | python3
- which command is the most compatible with Ubuntu Server (minimized) 22.04 and 24.04
For Ubuntu Server (minimized) 22.04 and 24.04, wget is the most compatible option.

Here's why:

wget is more compatible because:
Pre-installed: wget comes pre-installed on Ubuntu Server minimal installations by default
Ubuntu Server standard: It's part of the base Ubuntu Server package set
No additional dependencies: Works out of the box on fresh minimal installs
curl compatibility:
Not pre-installed: curl is NOT included in Ubuntu Server minimal installations by default
Requires installation: You'd need to run sudo apt install curl first
Additional dependency: Adds another package to the system
Recommended command:
wget -O - https://your-domain.com/remaster.py | python3
Verification:
You can verify this by checking what's available on a fresh Ubuntu Server minimal install:

wget âœ… (pre-installed)
curl âŒ (needs installation)
python3 âœ… (pre-installed)
So wget -O - is definitely the most compatible choice for Ubuntu Server 22.04 and 24.04 minimal installations.

i just made the PR public.. does that affect the main repo when we merge?
No, making the PR public does not affect the main repository's privacy settings when merged. Here's what happens:

PR Visibility vs Repository Visibility
PR Visibility: Controls who can see the pull request itself
Repository Visibility: Controls who can see the entire repository
When you merge the PR:
The main repository remains private (unchanged)
Only the PR becomes public (temporary)
After merging, the PR is closed but the repo stays private
The files from your PR branch get merged into the private main repo
Current situation:
Your PR branch is now public (temporary)
Main repo is still private
After merge: main repo stays private, PR closes
To access the file now:
Since your PR branch is public, you can use:

wget -O - https://raw.githubusercontent.com/MachoDrone/NosanaApplianceOS/cursor/create-remaster-py-with-version-control-5dde/remaster.py | python3
This should work now that the PR branch is public. The main repository's privacy is completely unaffected by the PR's visibility setting.

Ubuntu 22.04 build ISO (Both: MBR and EFI )
Asked 3 years, 2 months ago
Modified 2 months ago
Viewed 17k times
5

In our company we use a modified Ubuntu installation ISO image. Since Ubuntu 22.04 has changed the structure of the installation image (ISOLINUX has been removed, in favor of GRUB2), I would need advice on how to build a new ISO that would support both MBR and EFI at the same time (on same image), as Ubuntu 22.04.

In Ubuntu 20.04, I built the ISO image using the commands below.

So the question is: How to build an ISO image for Ubuntu 22.04? Thanks.

Commands for create ISO image with Ubuntu 20.04 LTS
Extract content from image:
dd if="/opt/ubnt/ubuntu-20.04.4-desktop-amd64.iso" bs=1 count=432 of="/opt/ubnt/isohdpfx.bin"
xorriso -osirrox on -indev /opt/ubnt/ubuntu-20.04.4-desktop-amd64.iso -extract / /opt/ubnt/ubuntu-MODIF
... Some customizations here ...

Build Ubuntu 20.04 LTS
xorriso -as mkisofs -r  
  -V 'Ubuntu 20.04 LTS MODIF (EFIBIOS)' 
  -o /opt/ubnt/ubuntu-modif.iso 
  -isohybrid-mbr /opt/ubnt/isohdpfx.bin 
  -J -joliet-long -b isolinux/isolinux.bin 
  -c isolinux/boot.cat 
  -boot-load-size 4 
  -boot-info-table -no-emul-boot -eltorito-alt-boot 
  -e boot/grub/efi.img 
  -no-emul-boot 
  -isohybrid-gpt-basdat /opt/ubnt/ubuntu-MODIF
bootgrub2uefiisocustomization

Ubuntu 22.04 build ISO (Both: MBR and EFI )

The youngest release of xorriso (1.5.4) can tell:


$ xorriso -indev ubuntu-22.04-desktop-amd64.iso -report_el_torito as_mkisofs
-V 'Ubuntu 22.04 LTS amd64'
--modification-date='2022041910231900'
--grub2-mbr --interval:local_fs:0s-15s:zero_mbrpt,zero_gpt:'ubuntu-22.04-desktop-amd64.iso'
--protective-msdos-label
-partition_cyl_align off
-partition_offset 16
--mbr-force-bootable
-append_partition 2 28732ac11ff8d211ba4b00a0c93ec93b --interval:local_fs:7129428d-7137923d::'ubuntu-22.04-desktop-amd64.iso'
-appended_part_as_gpt
-iso_mbr_part_type a2a0d0ebe5b9334487c068b6b72699c7
-c '/boot.catalog'
-b '/boot/grub/i386-pc/eltorito.img'
-no-emul-boot
-boot-load-size 4
-boot-info-table
--grub2-boot-info
-eltorito-alt-boot
-e '--interval:appended_partition_2_start_1782357s_size_8496d:all::'
-no-emul-boot
-boot-load-size 8496
Older versions guess wrong. The new layout surprises them somewhat. But they can create it. The original was made by xorriso-1.5.2.

The shown options are for reproducing the ISO as exactly as possible. For a modified ISO one will want to omit some of them to get default settings and replace others by actions which later don't need the presence of the original ISO image.


# Extract the MBR template for --grub2-mbr
# We only need the x86 code. All partition stuff will be newly created.
dd if=ubuntu-22.04-desktop-amd64.iso bs=1 count=432 of=/opt/ubnt/boot_hybrid.img

# The EFI partition is not a data file inside the ISO any more.
# So extract the EFI partition image image for -append_partition
# 7129428d-7137923d : 7137923 - 7129428 + 1 = 8496
dd if=ubuntu-22.04-desktop-amd64.iso bs=512 skip=7129428 count=8496 of=/opt/ubnt/efi.img

# Extract file tree as usual ...

# Finally pack up an ISO the new way
xorriso -as mkisofs -r \
  -V 'Ubuntu 22.04 LTS MODIF (EFIBIOS)' \
  -o /opt/ubnt/ubuntu-modif.iso \
  --grub2-mbr /opt/ubnt/boot_hybrid.img \
  -partition_offset 16 \
  --mbr-force-bootable \
  -append_partition 2 28732ac11ff8d211ba4b00a0c93ec93b /opt/ubnt/efi.img \
  -appended_part_as_gpt \
  -iso_mbr_part_type a2a0d0ebe5b9334487c068b6b72699c7 \
  -c '/boot.catalog' \
  -b '/boot/grub/i386-pc/eltorito.img' \
    -no-emul-boot -boot-load-size 4 -boot-info-table --grub2-boot-info \
  -eltorito-alt-boot \
  -e '--interval:appended_partition_2:::' \
    -no-emul-boot \
  /opt/ubnt/ubuntu-MODIF
The advantage is that the ISO on USB stick will be recognized as GPT partitioned with neatly separated partitions.


$ /sbin/fdisk -l ubuntu-22.04-desktop-amd64.iso

Disk ubuntu-22.04-desktop-amd64.iso: 3.4 GiB, 3654957056 bytes, 7138588 sectors
Units: sectors of 1 * 512 = 512 bytes
Sector size (logical/physical): 512 bytes / 512 bytes
I/O size (minimum/optimal): 512 bytes / 512 bytes
Disklabel type: gpt
Disk identifier: A09DB2B8-B5F6-43AE-AFB3-91E0A90189A1

Device                            Start     End Sectors  Size Type
ubuntu-22.04-desktop-amd64.iso1      64 7129427 7129364  3.4G Microsoft basic da
ubuntu-22.04-desktop-amd64.iso2 7129428 7137923    8496  4.2M EFI System
ubuntu-22.04-desktop-amd64.iso3 7137924 7138523     600  300K Microsoft basic da
(Partition 3 covers the traditional end padding of 300 KiB which is actually needed only on CD media written by write type Track-At-Once. Option -no-pad would prevent it.)

Thomas, how had you converted 7129428d-7137923d to 7137923 - 7129428 + 1 = 8496? â€“ 
Andrey Zentavr
 CommentedJul 27, 2022 at 2:33
1
Another question is: why you copy 432 bytes of MBR instead of 446? Classic MBR has 446 bytes of the bootstrap code area. Modern MBR has 218 bytes of Bootstrap code area (part 1) and 216 (or 222) of Bootstrap code area (part 2). â€“ 
Andrey Zentavr
 CommentedJul 27, 2022 at 2:44
In the xorrisofs documentation you can see that 'd' means multiply by 512 and since (7129428d-7137923d) tells us the start and end sector we must include the start sector in the calculation, so we do +1. In dd we are already operating on the number of sectors, so we skip 7129428 sectors and read the next 8496. â€“ 
Rabe
 CommentedApr 29 at 9:42

Great catch! The error is because the downloaded ubuntu-22.04.2-desktop-amd64.iso is only 282 bytesâ€”this means the download failed (likely a 404 or HTML error page, not the real ISO).

Why did this happen?

The script tried to download the ISO from https://releases.ubuntu.com/22.04/ubuntu-22.04.2-desktop-amd64.iso
That URL is either invalid, moved, or temporarily unavailable, so you got a tiny file (not a real ISO)
fdisk cannot operate on a non-ISO file, hence the error
How to Fix
Check the real ISO URL
The correct URL for Ubuntu 22.04.2 desktop ISO may have changed or may not be available anymore.

The official Ubuntu releases page is: https://releases.ubuntu.com/22.04/
The ISO may be named differently or may not be available for 22.04.2 specifically.
Add a download size check
The script should verify the file size after download and abort if it's suspiciously small (e.g., <100MB).

Fail gracefully
If the download fails or the file is not a valid ISO, print a clear error and exit.

Next Steps
I will update the script to:
Check the HTTP status code before writing the file.
Check the file size after download and abort if it's too small.
Print a clear error if the ISO is not valid.
(Optionally) Let you specify the ISO URL as an argument for flexibility.
I will make these changes and push them immediately.

You are absolutely right: the script must remove work_2204 after the ISO is built, and it must do so robustly (using shutil.rmtree or, if needed, sudo rm -rf as a fallback).

What I am doing with the hellow files:
HelloNOS.ESP:

Injected into the ESP (EFI System Partition) area of the new hybrid ISO.
This is done by appending the string Hello from HelloNOS.ESP! to the efi.img file before it is used in the ISO build.
Purpose: To test that you can inject a file into the EFI partition of a hybrid ISO and later verify its presence.
HelloNOS.BOOT:

Injected into the MBR/BOOT area of the new hybrid ISO.
This is done by appending the string Hello from HelloNOS.BOOT! to the boot_hybrid.img file before it is used in the ISO build.
Purpose: To test that you can inject a file into the MBR/BOOT area and later verify its presence.
HelloNOS.OPT:

Placed in the opt directory of the extracted ISO tree.
This is done by creating a file HelloNOS.OPT in the work_2204/opt/ directory before the ISO is rebuilt.
Purpose: To test that you can inject a file into the live filesystem of the ISO, so it is present on the installed system.
Why:
These files are test markers to verify that file injection into all three areas (ESP, MBR/BOOT, and live filesystem) works as expected in the remastered ISO.
They are only injected if the -hellow argument is provided.

I will update the script to use shutil.rmtree and, if that fails, fall back to sudo rm -rf to guarantee work_2204 is always removed unless -dc is set.
I will make this change and push it immediately.

Thank you for your direct feedback and for pointing out the persistent issue.

1. Why work_2204 is not being removed:
When running the script as wget -O - ... | python3, the script is executed via stdin, so __file__ is not set, and sometimes permissions or process context can interfere with cleanup.
shutil.rmtree and even sudo rm -rf may not work as expected if the script is not run as root or if the directory is locked by another process, or if the script is running as a piped stdin process (which can sometimes cause issues with permissions or process context).
2. The command you want:
To pass arguments to a script run via wget ... | python3, you must use the - to tell Python to read from stdin, and then add your arguments after the -:

wget -O - https://raw.githubusercontent.com/MachoDrone/NosanaApplianceOS/cursor/create-remaster-py-with-version-control-5dde/remaster.py | python3 - -hello
or, for both arguments:

wget -O - https://raw.githubusercontent.com/MachoDrone/NosanaApplianceOS/cursor/create-remaster-py-with-version-control-5dde/remaster.py | python3 - -hello -dc
Explanation:

The first - after python3 tells Python to execute the script from stdin.
Any arguments after that are passed to the script as sys.argv.
3. Immediate Fix for Cleanup
I will update the script to:

Always use subprocess.run(["sudo", "rm", "-rf", path]) as the final fallback for any directory that still exists after shutil.rmtree.
Print an error and exit if the directory still exists after all attempts.
I will make this change and push it immediately.

Since I'm having issues with the tool calls, let me provide you with the fix directly. The problem is in the mount/unmount handling. Here's what needs to be changed:

Version 0.02.0 Fix
The issue is that the _iso_mount directory is not being properly unmounted before cleanup. Here's the key fix needed:

Add a robust unmount function:
def force_unmount(path):
    """Force unmount a directory with multiple attempts"""
    if not os.path.exists(path):
        return True
    
    # Check if it's actually mounted
    try:
        result = subprocess.run(["mountpoint", "-q", path], capture_output=True)
        if result.returncode != 0:
            return True  # Not mounted
    except:
        pass
    
    print(f"Unmounting {path}...")
    # Try regular unmount first
    for attempt in range(3):
        result = subprocess.run(["sudo", "umount", path], capture_output=True)
        if result.returncode == 0:
            return True
        if attempt < 2:
            subprocess.run(["sleep", "1"])
    
    # Try force unmount
    result = subprocess.run(["sudo", "umount", "-f", path], capture_output=True)
    if result.returncode == 0:
        return True
    
    # Try lazy unmount as last resort
    result = subprocess.run(["sudo", "umount", "-l", path], capture_output=True)
    return result.returncode == 0
Update the verify_hello_files function:
def verify_hello_files(new_iso):
    # ... existing code ...
    finally:
        # Ensure proper unmount
        force_unmount(mount_point)
        
        # Clean up mount point directory
        if os.path.exists(mount_point):
            subprocess.run(["sudo", "rm", "-rf", mount_point], check=False)
Update the cleanup function:
def cleanup(temp_paths):
    for path in temp_paths:
        if os.path.isdir(path):
            # Force unmount if it's a mount point
            force_unmount(path)
            
            try:
                shutil.rmtree(path, ignore_errors=True)
            except Exception:
                pass
            if os.path.isdir(path):
                print(f"shutil.rmtree failed for {path}, trying sudo rm -rf")
                subprocess.run(["sudo", "rm", "-rf", path])
            if os.path.isdir(path):
                print(f"ERROR: Could not remove directory {path} after all attempts.")
        elif os.path.isfile(path):
            try:
                os.remove(path)
            except Exception:
                pass
Update the ensure_clean_dir function:
def ensure_clean_dir(path):
    if os.path.exists(path):
        force_unmount(path)
        subprocess.run(["sudo", "rm", "-rf", path])
    os.makedirs(path, exist_ok=True)
    subprocess.run(["sudo", "chown", f"{os.getuid()}:{os.getgid()}", path])

Version 0.02.1 Changes
Key Improvements:

Better mount point handling - Force cleanup any existing mount point before attempting to mount
Graceful "already mounted" handling - Detect when ISO is already mounted and continue verification
Improved error handling - Better error messages for mount operations
Simplified mount logic - Removed dependency on ensure_clean_dir in verification
More robust cleanup - Better cleanup of mount point directory
Technical Changes:

Added explicit check for "already mounted" error message
Force unmount and remove mount point before attempting new mount
Direct subprocess calls for better error handling
Improved error messages and flow control
The updated script is

now available at:

https://raw.githubusercontent.com/MachoDrone/NosanaApplianceOS/cursor/create-remaster-py-with-version-control-5dde/remaster.py
This should resolve the mount issues where the ISO was already mounted and causing the verification to fail. The script will now handle the case where the mount point is busy or already in use, and properly clean up before attempting to mount again.

The xorriso command now uses:

xorriso -as mkisofs -r -V 'NosanaAOS' -o NosanaAOS-0.24.04.2.iso \
  -J -l -c boot.catalog \
  -b boot/grub/i386-pc/eltorito.img -no-emul-boot -boot-load-size 4 -boot-info-table \
  -eltorito-alt-boot -e EFI/boot/bootx64.efi -no-emul-boot \
  -append_partition 2 0xef efi.img \
  -partition_cyl_align all \
  -isohybrid-mbr boot_hybrid.img \
  -isohybrid-gpt-basdat \
  working_dir

Perfect! The script is now working completely - all three verification tests are passing:

âœ… HelloNOS.OPT: PASS âœ… HelloNOS.ESP: PASS

Here are the commands to inspect the hello files:

1. Check HelloNOS.OPT in working_dir and mounted ISO:

# In working_dir
cat working_dir/opt/HelloNOS.OPT

# Mount ISO and check
sudo mkdir -p /mnt/iso
sudo mount -o loop NosanaAOS-0.24.04.2.iso /mnt/iso
cat /mnt/iso/opt/HelloNOS.OPT
sudo umount /mnt/iso
2. Check HelloNOS.ESP in efi.img:

# Check the efi.img file directly
hexdump -C efi.img | grep -A 2 -B 2 "Hello"
# Or search for the text
strings efi.img | grep HelloNOS
3. Check HelloNOS.BOOT in boot_hybrid.img:

# Check the boot_hybrid.img file directly
hexdump -C boot_hybrid.img | grep -A 2 -B 2 "Hello"
# Or search for the text
strings boot_hybrid.img | grep HelloNOS
4. Check HelloNOS.ESP and HelloNOS.BOOT in the final ISO:

# Check ESP in the ISO (first 10MB)
dd if=NosanaAOS-0.24.04.2.iso bs=1M count=10 | strings | grep HelloNOS

# Check BOOT in the ISO (first 2MB)

Ubuntu 22.04 build ISO (Both: MBR and EFI )
Asked 3 years, 2 months ago
Modified 2 months ago
Viewed 17k times
5

In our company we use a modified Ubuntu installation ISO image. Since Ubuntu 22.04 has changed the structure of the installation image (ISOLINUX has been removed, in favor of GRUB2), I would need advice on how to build a new ISO that would support both MBR and EFI at the same time (on same image), as Ubuntu 22.04.

In Ubuntu 20.04, I built the ISO image using the commands below.

So the question is: How to build an ISO image for Ubuntu 22.04? Thanks.

Commands for create ISO image with Ubuntu 20.04 LTS
Extract content from image:
dd if="/opt/ubnt/ubuntu-20.04.4-desktop-amd64.iso" bs=1 count=432 of="/opt/ubnt/isohdpfx.bin"
xorriso -osirrox on -indev /opt/ubnt/ubuntu-20.04.4-desktop-amd64.iso -extract / /opt/ubnt/ubuntu-MODIF
... Some customizations here ...

Build Ubuntu 20.04 LTS
xorriso -as mkisofs -r  
  -V 'Ubuntu 20.04 LTS MODIF (EFIBIOS)' 
  -o /opt/ubnt/ubuntu-modif.iso 
  -isohybrid-mbr /opt/ubnt/isohdpfx.bin 
  -J -joliet-long -b isolinux/isolinux.bin 
  -c isolinux/boot.cat 
  -boot-load-size 4 
  -boot-info-table -no-emul-boot -eltorito-alt-boot 
  -e boot/grub/efi.img 
  -no-emul-boot 
  -isohybrid-gpt-basdat /opt/ubnt/ubuntu-MODIF
bootgrub2uefiisocustomization

Ubuntu 22.04 build ISO (Both: MBR and EFI )

The youngest release of xorriso (1.5.4) can tell:


$ xorriso -indev ubuntu-22.04-desktop-amd64.iso -report_el_torito as_mkisofs
-V 'Ubuntu 22.04 LTS amd64'
--modification-date='2022041910231900'
--grub2-mbr --interval:local_fs:0s-15s:zero_mbrpt,zero_gpt:'ubuntu-22.04-desktop-amd64.iso'
--protective-msdos-label
-partition_cyl_align off
-partition_offset 16
--mbr-force-bootable
-append_partition 2 28732ac11ff8d211ba4b00a0c93ec93b --interval:local_fs:7129428d-7137923d::'ubuntu-22.04-desktop-amd64.iso'
-appended_part_as_gpt
-iso_mbr_part_type a2a0d0ebe5b9334487c068b6b72699c7
-c '/boot.catalog'
-b '/boot/grub/i386-pc/eltorito.img'
-no-emul-boot
-boot-load-size 4
-boot-info-table
--grub2-boot-info
-eltorito-alt-boot
-e '--interval:appended_partition_2_start_1782357s_size_8496d:all::'
-no-emul-boot
-boot-load-size 8496
Older versions guess wrong. The new layout surprises them somewhat. But they can create it. The original was made by xorriso-1.5.2.

The shown options are for reproducing the ISO as exactly as possible. For a modified ISO one will want to omit some of them to get default settings and replace others by actions which later don't need the presence of the original ISO image.


# Extract the MBR template for --grub2-mbr
# We only need the x86 code. All partition stuff will be newly created.
dd if=ubuntu-22.04-desktop-amd64.iso bs=1 count=432 of=/opt/ubnt/boot_hybrid.img

# The EFI partition is not a data file inside the ISO any more.
# So extract the EFI partition image image for -append_partition
# 7129428d-7137923d : 7137923 - 7129428 + 1 = 8496
dd if=ubuntu-22.04-desktop-amd64.iso bs=512 skip=7129428 count=8496 of=/opt/ubnt/efi.img

# Extract file tree as usual ...

# Finally pack up an ISO the new way
xorriso -as mkisofs -r \
  -V 'Ubuntu 22.04 LTS MODIF (EFIBIOS)' \
  -o /opt/ubnt/ubuntu-modif.iso \
  --grub2-mbr /opt/ubnt/boot_hybrid.img \
  -partition_offset 16 \
  --mbr-force-bootable \
  -append_partition 2 28732ac11ff8d211ba4b00a0c93ec93b /opt/ubnt/efi.img \
  -appended_part_as_gpt \
  -iso_mbr_part_type a2a0d0ebe5b9334487c068b6b72699c7 \
  -c '/boot.catalog' \
  -b '/boot/grub/i386-pc/eltorito.img' \
    -no-emul-boot -boot-load-size 4 -boot-info-table --grub2-boot-info \
  -eltorito-alt-boot \
  -e '--interval:appended_partition_2:::' \
    -no-emul-boot \
  /opt/ubnt/ubuntu-MODIF
The advantage is that the ISO on USB stick will be recognized as GPT partitioned with neatly separated partitions.


$ /sbin/fdisk -l ubuntu-22.04-desktop-amd64.iso

Disk ubuntu-22.04-desktop-amd64.iso: 3.4 GiB, 3654957056 bytes, 7138588 sectors
Units: sectors of 1 * 512 = 512 bytes
Sector size (logical/physical): 512 bytes / 512 bytes
I/O size (minimum/optimal): 512 bytes / 512 bytes
Disklabel type: gpt
Disk identifier: A09DB2B8-B5F6-43AE-AFB3-91E0A90189A1

Device                            Start     End Sectors  Size Type
ubuntu-22.04-desktop-amd64.iso1      64 7129427 7129364  3.4G Microsoft basic da
ubuntu-22.04-desktop-amd64.iso2 7129428 7137923    8496  4.2M EFI System
ubuntu-22.04-desktop-amd64.iso3 7137924 7138523     600  300K Microsoft basic da
(Partition 3 covers the traditional end padding of 300 KiB which is actually needed only on CD media written by write type Track-At-Once. Option -no-pad would prevent it.)

Thomas, how had you converted 7129428d-7137923d to 7137923 - 7129428 + 1 = 8496? â€“ 
Andrey Zentavr
 CommentedJul 27, 2022 at 2:33
1
Another question is: why you copy 432 bytes of MBR instead of 446? Classic MBR has 446 bytes of the bootstrap code area. Modern MBR has 218 bytes of Bootstrap code area (part 1) and 216 (or 222) of Bootstrap code area (part 2). â€“ 
Andrey Zentavr
 CommentedJul 27, 2022 at 2:44
In the xorrisofs documentation you can see that 'd' means multiply by 512 and since (7129428d-7137923d) tells us the start and end sector we must include the start sector in the calculation, so we do +1. In dd we are already operating on the number of sectors, so we skip 7129428 sectors and read the next 8496. â€“ 
Rabe

will the HelloNOS.OPT not carry over during the installation if I choose (minimized) install?
I'll research how Ubuntu's minimized installation affects the /opt directory and whether files placed there would persist during installation.

Searched the web 3 times
Based on my research, I can now provide you with a comprehensive answer about whether the `HelloN

OS.OPT` file will carry over during a minimized Ubuntu installation.

Analysis: HelloNOS.OPT File Persistence During Minimized Installation
The short answer is: No, the HelloNOS.OPT file will NOT carry over during either a minimized or standard Ubuntu installation.

Here's why:

How Ubuntu Installation Works
Live Environment vs. Installed System: The Ubuntu installation process works by:

Booting from the ISO into a "live" environment (temporary filesystem)
The installer then creates a completely new filesystem on the target disk
Files from the live environment are not copied to the installed system
Installation Process: During installation, Ubuntu:

Creates new partitions on the target disk
Installs packages from the ISO's package repository
Configures the system from scratch
Does NOT copy arbitrary files from the live filesystem to the installed system
The /opt Directory:

In the live environment, /opt is part of the temporary filesystem
During installation, a new /opt directory is created on the target disk
Files placed in /opt during the live session are not transferred
Minimized vs. Standard Installation
The difference between minimized and standard installation affects:

Package selection: Minimized installs fewer packages
System services: Fewer services are enabled
Applications: Fewer pre-installed applications
However, both installation types follow the same process of creating

Analysis: HelloNOS.OPT File Persistence During Minimized Installation

Does NOT copy arbitrary files from the live filesystem to the installed system

In the live environment, /opt is part of the temporary filesystem
During installation, a new /opt directory is created on the target disk
Files placed in /opt during the live session are not transferred
Minimized vs. Standard Installation
The difference between minimized and standard installation affects:

Package selection: Minimized installs fewer packages
System services: Fewer services are enabled
Applications: Fewer pre-installed applications
However, both installation types follow the same process of creating a fresh filesystem and not copying custom files from the live environment.

Your Test File Locations
From your remaster.py script, the three test files are injected at:

HelloNOS.ESP - EFI System Partition (offset 1024) âœ… Will persist - This is in the boot partition structure
HelloNOS.BOOT - MBR/boot area (offset 512) âœ… Will persist - This is in the boot sector
HelloNOS.OPT - /opt directory of ISO filesystem âŒ Will NOT persist - This is in the live environment only
Verification
Your script's verify_hello_files() function correctly verifies that all three files are present in the remastered ISO, but only the first two will survive the installation process because they're embedded in the boot structure, not the live filesystem.

Recommendations
If you need a file to persist after installation, you would need to:

Pre-seed it in the installer: Modify the Ubuntu installer itself to copy specific files
Use a post-install script: Include it in the installation automation (like cloud-init)
Embed it in a package: Create a custom package that gets installed
Place it in boot areas: Like you're already doing with the ESP and BOOT locations

The difference between minimized and standard installation affects:

Package selection: Minimized installs fewer packages
System services: Fewer services are enabled
Applications: Fewer pre-installed applications

can i find the other two HelloNOS files after installation?

Based on my research and understanding of how your remaster.py script works, here's where you can find the other two HelloNOS files after installation:

Finding HelloNOS Files After Installation
1. HelloNOS.ESP (EFI System Partition) âœ… ACCESSIBLE
Location: The EFI System Partition will be mounted at /boot/efi after installation.

How to find it:

# Check if EFI partition is mounted
mount | grep efi

# Navigate to the EFI partition
cd /boot/efi

# Look for your file (it was injected at offset 1024)
# You might need to use hexdump or dd to extract it
sudo hexdump -C /dev/sda1 | head -20  # Adjust device as needed

# Or use dd to extract the specific bytes
sudo dd if=/dev/sda1 bs=1 skip=1024 count=10 | hexdump -C
Note: Since your script injects HelloNOS.ESP at offset 1024 in the EFI partition, it should persist after installation because the EFI System Partition structure is preserved during the Ubuntu installation process.

Based on my research and understanding of how your remaster.py script works, here's where you can find the other two HelloNOS files after installation:

Finding HelloNOS Files After Installation
1. HelloNOS.ESP (EFI System Partition) âœ… ACCESSIBLE
Location: The EFI System Partition will be mounted at /boot/efi after installation.

How to find it:

# Check if EFI partition is mounted
mount | grep efi

# Navigate to the EFI partition
cd /boot/efi

# Look for your file (it was injected at offset 1024)
# You might need to use hexdump or dd to extract it
sudo hexdump -C /dev/sda1 | head -20  # Adjust device as needed

# Or use dd to extract the specific bytes
sudo dd if=/dev/sda1 bs=1 skip=1024 count=10 | hexdump -C
Note: Since your script injects HelloNOS.ESP at offset 1024 in the EFI partition, it should persist after installation because the EFI System Partition structure is preserved during the Ubuntu installation process.

2. HelloNOS.BOOT (MBR/Boot Sector) âœ… ACCESSIBLE
Location: This is in the Master Boot Record at offset 512 of the physical disk.

How to find it:

# View the MBR area where your file was injected
sudo hexdump -C /dev/sda | head -10  # Shows first part of disk

# Or specifically extract bytes starting at offset 512
sudo dd if=/dev/sda bs=1 skip=512 count=10 | hexdump -C

# You can also save it to a file for examination
sudo dd if=/dev/sda bs=1 skip=512 count=100 > hellonos_boot_area.bin
hexdump -C hellonos_boot_area.bin
Note: The MBR area (first 512 bytes) and the area immediately after it (where your file is at offset 512) should survive the installation process because Ubuntu preserves the boot sector structure.

3. HelloNOS.OPT (ISO Filesystem) âŒ NOT ACCESSIBLE
As discussed earlier, this file will not be accessible after installation because it was only in the live ISO filesystem.

Verification Script
You could create a simple verification script to run after installation:

#!/bin/bash
# verify_hellonos.sh

echo "Checking for HelloNOS files after installation..."

echo "1. Checking EFI partition for HelloNOS.ESP:"
if [ -d "/boot/efi" ]; then
    echo "EFI partition mounted at /boot/efi"
    # Extract bytes at offset 1024 to check for HelloNOS.ESP
    sudo dd if=/dev/sda1 bs=1 skip=1024 count=10 2>/dev/null | hexdump -C
else
    echo "EFI partition not found"
fi

echo -e "\n2. Checking MBR area for HelloNOS.BOOT:"
# Extract bytes at offset 512 to check for HelloNOS.BOOT
sudo dd if=/dev/sda bs=1 skip=512 count=10 2>/dev/null | hexdump -C

echo -e "\n3. Checking /opt for HelloNOS.OPT:"
if [ -f "/opt/HelloNOS.OPT" ]; then
    echo "Found HelloNOS.OPT in /opt"
    cat /opt/HelloNOS.OPT
else
    echo "HelloNOS.OPT not found in /opt (expected - this file doesn't persist)"
fi
Important Notes
Device Names: Replace /dev/sda and `/dev/sda

Verification Script
You could create a simple verification script to run after installation:

#!/bin/bash
# verify_hellonos.sh

echo "Checking for HelloNOS files after installation..."

echo "1. Checking EFI partition for HelloNOS.ESP:"
if [ -d "/boot/efi" ]; then
    echo "EFI partition mounted at /boot/efi"
    # Extract bytes at offset 1024 to check for HelloNOS.ESP
    sudo dd if=/dev/sda1 bs=1 skip=1024 count=10 2>/dev/null | hexdump -C
else
    echo "EFI partition not found"
fi

echo -e "\n2. Checking MBR area for HelloNOS.BOOT:"
# Extract bytes at offset 512 to check for HelloNOS.BOOT
sudo dd if=/dev/sda bs=1 skip=512 count=10 2>/dev/null | hexdump -C

echo -e "\n3. Checking /opt for HelloNOS.OPT:"
if [ -f "/opt/HelloNOS.OPT" ]; then
    echo "Found HelloNOS.OPT in /opt"
    cat /opt/HelloNOS.OPT
else
    echo "HelloNOS.OPT not found in /opt (expected - this file doesn't persist)"
fi
Important Notes
Device Names: Replace /dev/sda and /dev/sda1 with the actual device names on your system. You can find these using:

lsblk
fdisk -l
Permissions: You'll need sudo privileges to access raw disk devices.

Exact Offsets: The success of finding these files depends on the exact offsets used in your remaster.py script and whether the installation process preserves those specific areas.

EFI vs BIOS: The EFI file location depends on whether you're using UEFI or legacy BIOS boot mode.

The two files that should persist (HelloNOS.ESP and HelloNOS.BOOT) are in areas that are typically preserved during the Ubuntu installation process, so you should be able to find them using the methods above.
